--- Commit Message ---
LU-12275 sec: deal with encrypted object size

Problem with size of encrypted file comes from the fact that
an encrypted page will always contain PAGE_SIZE bytes of data,
even if clear text page is only a few bytes. And server infers
object size from content of encrypted page.

The way to address this is the following. Upon writing, when the
client encrypts the page representing the end of the file, it puts
into o_size info of the request's body, the size of the clear text
version of the file. On server side, this information is used to
adjust isize of the object, but still storing the complete pages
on disk.

Signed-off-by: Sebastien Buisson <sbuisson@ddn.com>

--- Message (Patrick Farrell, 2019-09-11 19:46:00.000000000) ---
Patch Set 3:

Well, consider that the size is a single value, while the writes themselves cover blocks and are racing.

If we assume a file that is 1K in size, then write 1 is a single 1K write at offset 1K, and write 2 is a single 1K write at offset 2K, then there is no conflict between writes 1 and 2.  But of course, the size is 3K at the end, so it is important that at the end, we have the size from write 2.  (Or, if we put write 1 at offset 2K and write 2 at offset 1K, it would be important that at the end we have the size from write 1.)

If the writes overlap and really are racing, then it is a legitimate race condition.

So if the writes overlap, say both at offset 1K, then which data is kept is a legitimate race.

Consider a case like this:
Both writes at offset 1K.

Write 1 is 1K in size, and is just "AAA[...]".  Write 2 is 2K in size, and is just "BBB[...]".  The resulting file size is of course 3K, but what is in the file from offset 1K to 2K?  The only guarantee is that it is either *ALL* from write 1 or all from write 2 (AAA, BBB).

This is assuming the two writes are truly occurring at the same time, ie, the syscalls overlap.

Well, in this case of 1KB writes would be handled at the client and be exclusive to a single node due to DLM locking.  A more common case for racing sizes at the server would be 4KB sized/aligned writes.

- store the actual file size on the object and use the same implicit

  the complexity of a separate xattr, but not the race condition with
  the size updates. 
- handle this as part of the normal write path doing i_size updates
  in osd_write_commit() using sizediff when isize is computed

--- Message (Andreas Dilger, 2019-09-12 01:51:08.000000000) ---
Patch Set 3:

> So what if we sent over the actual *size*...? And then we would
 > update this diff using the same rule as the size (ie, we only
 > update enc_diff when the file size is changed, meaning when the
 > file size *grows* [on writes, truncate of course has its own
 > separate handling]). If page size has to be the same on client &
 > server, we could send *just* the size, otherwise, we could send
 > the size & the diff, but only write the diff when the size would
 > change.

In essence we *already* send over the (potentially) new size with every write in the niobuf offset+length, which is what osd_write_commit() uses to determine the new file size.  I think that "all" we need to do is adjust this by sizediff as part of the BRW RPC, and store the new size directly on the inode.

We already have proper exclusion of i_size updates on the OST (via inode->i_lock spinlock), and luckily this is *not* a hot path because only one (or a few) threads are writing at end-of-file, and the majority of other threads are writing in the middle of the file.

The one caveat is that we would now be storing some data beyond i_size, but even for ZFS I think that would be OK, since the OST recordsize is always going to be at least a multiple of 4KB, and the "size" of a ZFS object (as opposed to the blocks) is a "higher user-level concept" that it defers management of to Lustre (or ZPL).

For 64KB PAGE_SIZE clients and 4KB block OSTs, together with my proposal that this be handled as 16 separate 4KB encryption chunks, we could still safely assume that any data past the end of the 4KB block overlapping i_size is zero and not send it to the OSTs.  That means we only ever need to write the full 4KB block at i_size to disk plus the actual file size, which is exactly what ext4/ldiskfs already does.

(5 comments)

I think I understand why the current implementation is racy, and in fact could lead to inconsistencies regarding file size vs file content. The fact that size diff is global to the object, whereas every write RPC could potentially think that it writes at the end of the file, in a context where requests can be processed out-of-order on server side, could lead to inconsistencies.

I also appreciate the drawbacks of using an xattr to store size diff. My idea was to limit the impact of this ‘encrypted file size handling’ code, and not go too deep in the Lustre server layers. But it might turn out to be suboptimal.

Maybe we should focus on the race condition first, as it could have consequences on the way to address the other aspects. I am note sure how osd_write_commit() could instruct the underlying file system to write n bytes, but to make it consider i_size is only m (with m <= n). Is it possible to set an arbitrary size on a file, independently from its content? I am not sure this is what you are suggesting, and I am not familiar with the osd layer, so I am just thinking out loud.

What about sending, along with o_enc_sizediff, the page index (the same index that is used as logical block number in calls to fscrypt_encrypt_page) as a ‘generation number’ mentioned by Patrick, and store it in the xattr along with the size diff? The page index would help finding out if the size diff reported in the request is actually the ‘latest’ one. So even if the requests are processed out-of-order by the server, we would know what is the correct value to store.
Hopefully, as Andreas mentioned, this is not a hot path because only a few threads are writing at the end of the file, the rest writing in the middle of the file.

Anyway, I think I really need to write tests for the encryption/decryption feature. It will be easier for development and testing of alternatives to this first implementation. I plan to push a separate patch for the tests, but I am wondering, do you know how to, with Lustre test framework, artificially create the race we are mentioning? Maybe with 2 clients and a fail loc on server side that makes request processing sleep somewhere, but only if the request comes from a given client?

--- Message (Patrick Farrell, 2019-09-17 20:40:31.000000000) ---
Patch Set 3:

(2 comments)

So in this note, I'm talking mostly about Sebastien's current implementation, but I think it's worth considering Andreas' suggestion - Maybe we can do exactly what ext4 encryption does and disconnect file size from block data (where we read all of the last block, etc, etc).  That seems like it might be doable, but it would definitely require some fiddling in the OSDs.  But if it can be done, then we save ourselves the trouble of an xattr.

Also, I haven't really raised this concern (yet), but one major issue is atomicity of the size update with the write.  I haven't looked at whether or not the existing mechanism guarantees we can't see the written data before the size_diff is updated.  If we're using the on disk file system to store size in the standard manner, we can guarantee size changes will be atomic by using the existing mechanism(s).

"Is it possible to set an arbitrary size on a file, independently from its content?"
Yes, this is what "truncate" (which is a setattr op) does.  "truncate" as a verb implies only reducing size, but the truncate operation can set file size to any value, including increasing it.

"What about sending, along with o_enc_sizediff, the page index (the same index that is used as logical block number in calls to fscrypt_encrypt_page) as a ‘generation number’ mentioned by Patrick, and store it in the xattr along with the size diff? The page index would help finding out if the size diff reported in the request is actually the ‘latest’ one. So even if the requests are processed out-of-order by the server, we would know what is the correct value to store."

I am not sure I fully understand this, but it's worth noting that we don't want the *latest* size, we want the *greatest* size.  It's possible for later writes to come earlier in the file (consider multiple writers, or random writes).

"Anyway, I think I really need to write tests for the encryption/decryption feature. It will be easier for development and testing of alternatives to this first implementation. I plan to push a separate patch for the tests, but I am wondering, do you know how to, with Lustre test framework, artificially create the race we are mentioning? Maybe with 2 clients and a fail loc on server side that makes request processing sleep somewhere, but only if the request comes from a given client?"

We're discussing a few different races/conflicts.  The simplest one, which isn't even really a race, is just where the first write is "deeper" in the file than a later one.  So write 1K at offset 5K, then write 1K at offset 3K from another client (this is a harder test than doing so from the same client).  You can simply wait for the first write syscall to return - No need for anything else.  Obviously file size should not change after the first write.

Really, as long as there is correct locking around file size changes, this is the only case that needs to be handled.

Basically, size can change in three ways:
It can go *down* from a truncate/setattr
It can go *up* from a truncate/setattr
It can go *up* when a write ends beyond current size

So truncate is special in that it can change size arbitrarily, but writes can only ever increase size.  So we only change size on writes when a write is beyond current size.

--- Message (Andreas Dilger, 2019-09-18 02:32:45.000000000) ---
Patch Set 3:

The code in osd-ldiskfs::osd_write_commit() is properly handling the inode->i_size update.  It computes isize (= last byte modified by this write), then stores it to inode->i_size and the disk inode under lock IFF it is larger than the current size.  The calculation of isize would be the appropriate place to adjust the size based on o_enc_offset.

Alternately, we can just pass the actual size in o_size along with the OST_BRW_WRITE RPC, and use that directly, but independent of how many pages are sent to the OST.  We already allow blocks beyond i_size in ldiskfs, and ZFS doesn't care about the size either.

--- Message (Sebastien Buisson, 2019-09-18 15:59:51.000000000) ---
Patch Set 3:

(1 comment)

My concern about changing i_size on the object is for subsequent reads. Let’s take a simple example by considering a clear text page that has only 32 valid bytes at the beginning (the rest of it being zeros). We have to encrypt the whole page (encryptionchunk size is PAGE_SIZE), which produces an encrypted page full of PAGE_SIZE data. Later on, in order to recover clear text data, we have to read the whole encrypted page (PAGE_SIZE bytes) and apply the decryption algorithm. This will produce PAGE_SIZE bytes of decrypted data: the first 32 valid bytes and then zeros. If you only read and decrypt the first 32 bytes from the encrypted page, the decryption will not produce the correct result (decryption chunk size is PAGE_SIZE).
So, back to adapting i_size on the object, upon later read it will only return i_size bytes to the upper layers, forgetting the end of the last page. This will result in incorrect decryption by the client. We could imagine a mechanism where the OSD layer always returns data as a multiple of client PAGE_SIZE (implying the server knows this info), but only for objects that belong to encrypted files, which means setting a special flag on the object when created (not sure how that’s possible). And as in current implementation, i_size would be used to adapt lvb_size returned to the client, letting the client know how many bytes are actual data.

Regarding the current patch, please be aware that size diff is only sent by the client when the last page in the object is not full. So we cannot simply end up storing the wrong size diff on the object in case of consecutive writes at offsets in "decreasing orders".

> We're discussing a few different races/conflicts.  The simplest one,
> which isn't even really a race, is just where the first write is "deeper" in
> the file than a later one.  So write 1K at offset 5K, then write 1K at offset
> 3K from another client (this is a harder test than doing so from the
> same client).  You can simply wait for the first write syscall to return -
> No need for anything else.  Obviously file size should not change after
> the first write."

Not sure to understand this case. Write at offset 5K should change size, and write at offset 3K should not, right? Unless you meant ‘second write’ instead of ‘first write’ in your last sentence? I do not see how the current patch could end up with the wrong file size in this scenario. Are the explanations above shedding a light on this?

I agree the current implementation cannot work with competing writes, when multiple clients writing at different offsets (multiple of PAGE_SIZE so that they do not compete for locks) all think they are writing at the end of the file. As mentioned in a previous comment, I tried changing the current implementation by making the client also send the page index along with the size diff. In this case the server is able to discard size diff if it does not report to the *greatest* page index (sorry, my use of ‘latest’ was completely wrong and confusing). In fact sending size diff + page index has completely the same effect as sending the object size alone, as long as the server only takes the greatest one into account. But it also has the same drawback when relying on a xattr to remember this value: it has to be updated for every write request at end of object. Unfortunately, applications appending data at end of file is quite a common use case. So I have not pushed this new version here, because it might completely kill performance.

To sum up my thoughts, 1) implementation based on the use of an xattr seems too costly in order to guarantee coherency. And 2) implementation based on file size handling at the OSD layer would need more work than initially thought for the read path. Do you agree with performance conclusions on 1) ? Can you see a way to implement proper read path in 2) ?

--- Message (Sebastien Buisson, 2019-09-18 17:04:58.000000000) ---
Patch Set 3:

Just to add a comment about implementation based on the use of an xattr. It the client sends the greatest page index along with the size diff, it is possible to avoid setting the page index in the xattr (so just set the size diff when it changes). But the server needs to keep track of the greatest page index in the ofd_object structure, which means there is still the need for a write lock on the variable, even if we do not do a setxattr every time a client writes at the end of an object. That would save time, but maybe still taking a write lock would hurt performance significantly. Not sure what would be the best access pattern (and benchmark) to measure this.

--- Message (Andreas Dilger, 2019-09-18 21:19:46.000000000) ---
Patch Set 3:

> Just to add a comment about implementation based on the use of an
 > xattr. It the client sends the greatest page index along with the
 > size diff, it is possible to avoid setting the page index in the
 > xattr (so just set the size diff when it changes). But the server
 > needs to keep track of the greatest page index in the ofd_object
 > structure, which means there is still the need for a write lock on
 > the variable, even if we do not do a setxattr every time a client
 > writes at the end of an object. That would save time, but maybe
 > still taking a write lock would hurt performance significantly. Not
 > sure what would be the best access pattern (and benchmark) to
 > measure this.

We already lock the file size update during write:
- osd-ldiskfs::osd_write_commit() uses spin_lock(&inode->i_lock)
- osd-zfs::osd_write_commit() uses write_lock(&obj->oo_attr_lock)

so I don't think the atomicity of the update will be a problem.  My main concern is that storing the file size in an xattr is easy to do things incorrectly, and this adds code complexity.

As for the clients accessing e.g. a 32-byte encrypted file with 4096 bytes of encrypted data, I think this is something that the client needs to handle itself.  If the file is encrypted, then it should request full PAGE_SIZE (or "multiple of 4KB" reads), regardless of what the file size is.

--- Message (Sebastien Buisson, 2019-09-20 16:43:04.000000000) ---

I will push a new version of this patch. It consists in taking on server side the size diff info as received by the client, and adjusting accordingly the size of the object, but still making sure that complete pages are committed to disk.

--- Message (Andreas Dilger, 2020-03-27 03:50:42.000000000) ---
Patch Set 16: Code-Review+1

It would be useful to verify (at least once manually) if you mount an ldiskfs OST directly that the file content can be decrypted with the ext4 fscrypt implementation.

--- Message (Sebastien Buisson, 2020-04-08 07:24:32.000000000) ---
Patch Set 16:

> It would be useful to verify (at least once manually) if you mount
 > an ldiskfs OST directly that the file content can be decrypted with
 > the ext4 fscrypt implementation.

That might not be really feasible to mount an ldiskfs OST directly and check that file content can be decrypted with the ext4 fscrypt implementation. Indeed, on OSTs, nothing really tells that files are encrypted except a flag, and the actual encryption information is stored on the inode on MDT.
